### [294] [Predictability as a Fine-Grained Measure for Privacy](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20546)
*Linda Lu,Karthik Sridharan*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper introduces "privacy via predictability," a fine-grained privacy framework that measures leakage as the incremental gain in attacker's ability to predict sensitive information after seeing algorithm output, beyond what compromised data already reveals. It shows predictability and DP are generally incomparable but can connect in worst-case regimes, and provides a GMM-based analysis for asymptotic predictability with a calibrated perturbation scheme for ERM.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Differential privacy's worst-case nature imposes a costly privacy-accuracy tradeoff. The authors aim to provide a finer-grained framework that explicitly incorporates attacker's core knowledge and specific sensitive information, enabling more tailored privacy guarantees that can be less costly than DP's worst-case approach.

Method: The paper defines predictability as the incremental gain in attacker's prediction ability of sensitive information after observing algorithm output, given compromised data from a stochastic process. It provides theoretical comparison with DP, develops a GMM-based asymptotic analysis for stationary ergodic mixing processes, and derives a predictability-calibrated output perturbation scheme for empirical risk minimization (ERM).

Result: Predictability and DP are generally incomparable (each can be small while the other is large). In the worst-case regime where all but one individual is compromised and all binary queries are sensitive, predictability implies mutual-information DP. The GMM framework enables asymptotic predictability analysis, leading to a calibrated perturbation scheme for ERM.

Conclusion: Privacy via predictability offers a complementary, finer-grained privacy metric tailored to specific attacker models and sensitive information, which can be used alongside DP to provide more nuanced privacy control without the full cost of worst-case DP guarantees.

Abstract: Differential privacy (DP) ensures rigorous individual-level privacy guarantees against even the most knowledgeable attackers, but its worst-case nature can impose a costly privacy-accuracy tradeoff. We introduce privacy via predictability, a fine-grained framework that explicitly incorporates the attacker's core knowledge, a compromised portion of the dataset generated by a stochastic process, and a specified family of queries. Predictability measures privacy leakage as the incremental gain in an attacker's ability to predict sensitive information about unknown individuals after observing the algorithm's output, beyond what can already be inferred from the compromised data. We show that predictability and DP are generally incomparable: each can be small while the other is large. However, in the worst-case regime where all but one individual is compromised, and all binary queries are considered sensitive, predictability implies mutual-information DP. More generally, predictability provides a finer-grained privacy metric tailored to specific sensitive information and specific attacker models. We introduce a general framework, using the generalized method of moments (GMM), to analyze asymptotic predictability when the compromised data is generated by a stationary, ergodic, mixing process. Using this analysis, we derive a predictability-calibrated output perturbation scheme for ERM. Our approach is complementary to DP and can be used alongside DP to provide fine-grained privacy control.

</details>


### [295] [Optimal Deterministic Multicalibration and Omniprediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20557)
*Georgy Noarov,Aaron Roth*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: This paper resolves whether randomization is necessary for optimal sample complexity in multicalibration, showing a deterministic predictor achieves the minimax-optimal $\widetilde O(\varepsilon^{-3})$ rate, and extends this to outcome indistinguishability, omnipredictors, and panpredictors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work achieved minimax-optimal $\widetilde O(\varepsilon^{-3})$ sample complexity for $\varepsilon$-multicalibration only with randomized predictors; deterministic predictors had substantially worse rates. Whether randomization is necessary for optimal sample complexity was an open question posed by [CLNR26] and earlier works.

Method: The authors develop a minimax-optimal multicalibration algorithm that outputs a deterministic predictor. They then generalize this algorithm to produce optimal deterministic predictors satisfying outcome indistinguishability for finite or finitely covered test collections, and apply it to omnipredictors and panpredictors.

Result: A deterministic predictor achieves the minimax-optimal $\widetilde O(\varepsilon^{-3})$ sample complexity for multicalibration, and the generalized algorithm yields optimal deterministic predictors for OI, omnipredictors, and panpredictors.

Conclusion: Randomization is not necessary for optimal sample complexity in multicalibration, resolving open problems from [CLNR26], [OKK25], and [BHHLZ25].

Abstract: A model is multicalibrated on a collection of group weights $G$ if it is calibrated -- i.e. unbiased even conditional on its prediction -- not just overall, but also after reweighting contexts by each $g \in G$. It is a useful property for many downstream applications and is a basic desideratum of trustworthy machine learning. Before this work, all predictors known to attain the minimax-optimal $\widetilde O(\varepsilon^{-3})$ sample complexity rate for $\varepsilon$-multicalibration were randomized, while deterministic predictors were known only with substantially worse sample complexity. Whether randomization is necessary for optimal sample complexity in multicalibration was explicitly asked by [CLNR26] and implicitly in several prior works.
  We resolve this open problem by giving a minimax-optimal multicalibration algorithm that outputs a deterministic predictor. We then generalize the algorithm to produce optimal deterministic predictors that satisfy outcome indistinguishability (OI) with respect to finite or finitely covered collections of tests. As an application, this also gives deterministic omnipredictors and panpredictors with optimal sample complexity, resolving open problems posed by [OKK25] and [BHHLZ25].

</details>


### [296] [How Transparent is DiffusionGemma?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20560)
*Joshua Engels,Callum McDougall,Bilal Chughtai,Janos Kramar,Senthoran Rajamanoharan,Cindy Wu,Arthur Conmy,Asic Q Chen,Jean Tarbouriech,Min Ma,Brendan O'Donoghue,João Gabriel Lopes de Oliveira,Rohin Shah,Neel Nanda*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: DiffusionGemma's reasoning transparency is studied by decomposing it into variable and algorithmic transparency. Despite initially appearing 28.6X less transparent than Gemma 4 due to opaque serial depth, mapping information through an interpretable token bottleneck reduces this to 1.1X. Algorithmic transparency is lower due to distributed computation across denoising steps, but novel phenomena (non-chronological reasoning, token/sequence smearing, intermediate-context reasoning) are identified. Monitorability is comparable to Gemma 4.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To assess whether DiffusionGemma's continuous latent space computation reduces reasoning transparency compared to autoregressive models like Gemma 4, which is critical for understanding model decisions, mitigating misuse/misalignment, and debugging surprising behaviors.

Method: Decomposes transparency into variable transparency (understanding intermediate computational states) and algorithmic transparency (reconstructing the process from these states). Uses an interpretable token bottleneck between denoising steps. Conducts interpretability case studies to identify diffusion-specific phenomena. Tests monitorability for downstream task utility.

Result: Initial opaque serial depth is 28.6X higher than Gemma 4, but mapping through an interpretable token bottleneck reduces it to 1.1X. Novel diffusion-specific phenomena identified: non-chronological reasoning, token/sequence smearing, intermediate-context reasoning. Monitorability is similar to Gemma 4.

Conclusion: Despite challenges, DiffusionGemma's variable transparency can be effectively bridged via token bottleneck mapping, though algorithmic transparency remains harder for diffusion models. The model is similarly monitorable to autoregressive counterparts.

Abstract: LLM reasoning transparency is a critical affordance for understanding model decisions, mitigating misuse and misalignment, and debugging surprising model behaviors. However, DiffusionGemma performs a larger fraction of its computation in a continuous latent space; does this make its reasoning less transparent? We study this question by decomposing transparency into two components: variable transparency, whether we understand intermediate snapshots of a model's computational state; and algorithmic transparency, whether we can use these snapshots to reconstruct the process by which the model arrived at its outputs. Naively, DiffusionGemma has poor variable transparency: its opaque serial depth, the amount of serial computation that occurs in between interpretable model states, seems at first 28.6X higher than the corresponding autoregressive Gemma 4 model. However, we show that we can map the information flowing between denoising steps through an interpretable token bottleneck with no decrease in downstream performance. Treating these intermediate states as interpretable reduces the opaque serial depth to just 1.1X that of Gemma 4. Algorithmic transparency is harder for diffusion models than for autoregressive models because all token predictions in the canvas can change at every denoising step, giving the model the power to implement complicated distributed algorithms during the denoising process. To begin bridging this gap, we conduct a suite of interpretability case studies, uncovering initial evidence of novel diffusion-specific phenomena such as non-chronological reasoning, token and sequence smearing, and intermediate-context reasoning. Finally, we test monitorability, a key application of transparency that measures whether model outputs are useful for downstream tasks. We find that DiffusionGemma is similarly monitorable to Gemma 4.

</details>
